</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Economic_sectors" title="Template:Economic sectors"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Economic_sectors" title="Template talk:Economic sectors"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Economic_sectors" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Economic sectors"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1246091330" /><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks" role="navigation"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Secondary_sector" title="Category:Secondary sector">a series</a> of articles on</td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle"><a href="/wiki/Machine_industry" title="Machine industry">Machine industry</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Factory.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Mill"><img alt="Mill" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Factory.svg/120px-Factory.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="87" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Factory.svg/250px-Factory.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="502" data-file-height="438" /></a></span></td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Machine_industry" title="Template:Machine industry"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Machine_industry" title="Template talk:Machine industry"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Machine_industry" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Machine industry"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Manufacturing</b> is the creation or <a href="/wiki/Production_(economics)" title="Production (economics)">production</a> of <a href="/wiki/Goods" title="Goods">goods</a> with the help of equipment, <a href="/wiki/Work_(human_activity)" title="Work (human activity)">labor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological processing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Formulation" title="Formulation">formulation</a>. It is the essence of the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sector_of_the_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary sector of the economy">secondary sector of the economy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2023)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The term may refer to a range of <a href="/wiki/Human_behavior" title="Human behavior">human activity</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Handicraft" title="Handicraft">handicraft</a> to <a href="/wiki/High_tech_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="High tech manufacturing">high-tech</a>, but it is most commonly applied to <a href="/wiki/Industrial_design" title="Industrial design">industrial design</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw materials</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Primary_sector_of_the_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary sector of the economy">primary sector</a> are transformed into <a href="/wiki/Finished_good" class="mw-redirect" title="Finished good">finished goods</a> on a large scale. Such goods may be sold to other manufacturers for the production of other more complex products (such as aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Major_appliance" title="Major appliance">household appliances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sports_equipment" title="Sports equipment">sports equipment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Automobiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobiles">automobiles</a>), or distributed via the <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Tertiary industry">tertiary industry</a> to <a href="/wiki/End_user" title="End user">end users</a> and consumers (usually through wholesalers, who in turn sell to retailers, who then sell them to individual customers).
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_on_work_stand.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/New_Horizons_on_work_stand.jpg/250px-New_Horizons_on_work_stand.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/New_Horizons_on_work_stand.jpg/500px-New_Horizons_on_work_stand.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Manufacturing of <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons space probe</a> at <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA Research Laboratory</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_engineering" title="Manufacturing engineering">Manufacturing engineering</a> is the field of engineering that designs and optimizes the <b>manufacturing process</b>, or the steps through which raw materials are transformed into a <a href="/wiki/Final_product" title="Final product">final product</a>. The manufacturing process begins with <a href="/wiki/Product_design" title="Product design">product design</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_materials" title="Bill of materials">materials specification</a>. These materials are then modified through manufacturing to become the desired product.
</p><p>Contemporary manufacturing encompasses all intermediary stages involved in producing and integrating components of a product. Some industries, such as <a href="/wiki/Fabrication_(semiconductor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fabrication (semiconductor)">semiconductor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fabrication_(metal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fabrication (metal)">steel</a> manufacturers, use the term <b>fabrication</b> instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The manufacturing sector is closely connected with the engineering and industrial design industries.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> word <i>manufacture</i> is likely derived from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/manufacture#Middle_French" class="extiw" title="wikt:manufacture">manufacture</a></i> ("process of making") which itself originates from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/manu#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:manu">manū</a></i> ("hand") and the Middle French <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/facture" class="extiw" title="wikt:facture">facture</a></i> ("making"). Alternatively, the English word may have been independently formed from the earlier English <i>manufacture</i> ("made by human hands") and <i>fracture</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its earliest usage in the English language was recorded in the mid-16th century to refer to the making of products by hand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History_and_development">History and development</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History and development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Prehistory_and_ancient_history">Prehistory and ancient history</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory and ancient history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Industry_(archaeology)" title="Industry (archaeology)">Industry (archaeology)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_technology" title="Prehistoric technology">Prehistoric technology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_technology" title="Ancient technology">Ancient technology</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Stone_Core_for_Making_Blades_-_Boqer_Tachtit,_Negev,_circa_40000_BP_(detail).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Stone_Core_for_Making_Blades_-_Boqer_Tachtit%2C_Negev%2C_circa_40000_BP_%28detail%29.jpg/250px-Stone_Core_for_Making_Blades_-_Boqer_Tachtit%2C_Negev%2C_circa_40000_BP_%28detail%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Stone_Core_for_Making_Blades_-_Boqer_Tachtit%2C_Negev%2C_circa_40000_BP_%28detail%29.jpg/500px-Stone_Core_for_Making_Blades_-_Boqer_Tachtit%2C_Negev%2C_circa_40000_BP_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3924" data-file-height="2787" /></a><figcaption>Flint stone core for making blades in <a href="/wiki/Negev" title="Negev">Negev</a>, Israel, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 40000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sword_bronze_age_(2nd_version).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg/250px-Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg/500px-Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="802" /></a><figcaption>A late <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_sword" title="Bronze Age sword">Bronze Age sword</a> or dagger blade now on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Archaeological_Museum,_France" title="National Archaeological Museum, France">National Archaeological Museum</a> in France</figcaption></figure>
<p>Human ancestors manufactured objects using stone and other tools long before the emergence of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> about 200,000 years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The earliest methods of <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tool</a> making, known as the <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> "<a href="/wiki/Industry_(archaeology)" title="Industry (archaeology)">industry</a>", date back to at least 2.3&#160;million years ago,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with the earliest direct evidence of tool usage found in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley,_Kenya" title="Great Rift Valley, Kenya">Great Rift Valley</a>, dating back to 2.5&#160;million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To manufacture a stone tool, a "<a href="/wiki/Lithic_core" title="Lithic core">core</a>" of hard stone with specific flaking properties (such as <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a>) was struck with a <a href="/wiki/Hammerstone" title="Hammerstone">hammerstone</a>. This flaking produced sharp edges that could be used as tools, primarily in the form of <a href="/wiki/Chopper_(archaeology)" title="Chopper (archaeology)">choppers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scraper_(archaeology)" title="Scraper (archaeology)">scrapers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ea&#95;archaeology&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ea_archaeology-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These tools greatly aided the early humans in their <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> lifestyle to form other tools out of softer materials such as bone and wood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a>, approximately 300,000 years ago, saw the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Prepared-core_technique" title="Prepared-core technique">prepared-core technique</a>, where multiple blades could be rapidly formed from a single core stone.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ea&#95;archaeology&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ea_archaeology-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pressure_flaking" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressure flaking">Pressure flaking</a>, in which a wood, bone, or antler <a href="/wiki/Punch_(engineering)" class="mw-redirect" title="Punch (engineering)">punch</a> could be used to shape a stone very finely was developed during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, beginning approximately 40,000 years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period, polished <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tools</a> were manufactured from a variety of hard rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jadeite" title="Jadeite">jadeite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greenschist" title="Greenschist">greenstone</a>. The polished axes were used alongside other stone tools including <a href="/wiki/Projectile_point" title="Projectile point">projectiles</a>, knives, and scrapers, as well as tools manufactured from organic materials such as wood, bone, and antler.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Copper <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> is believed to have originated when the technology of pottery <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> allowed sufficiently high temperatures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tylecote&#95;1992&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tylecote_1992-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The concentration of various elements such as arsenic increases with depth in copper ore deposits and smelting of these ores yields <a href="/wiki/Arsenical_bronze" title="Arsenical bronze">arsenical bronze</a>, which can be sufficiently work-hardened to be suitable for manufacturing tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tylecote&#95;1992&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tylecote_1992-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronze</a> is an alloy of copper with tin; the latter of which being found in relatively few deposits globally delayed true tin bronze from becoming widespread. During the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, bronze was a major improvement over stone as a material for making tools, both because of its mechanical properties like strength and ductility and because it could be cast in molds to make intricately shaped objects. Bronze significantly advanced shipbuilding technology with better tools and bronze nails, which replaced the old method of attaching boards of the hull with cord woven through drilled holes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> is conventionally defined by the widespread manufacturing of weapons and tools using iron and steel rather than bronze.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-waldbaum&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waldbaum-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Iron smelting is more difficult than tin and copper smelting because smelted iron requires hot-working and can be melted only in specially designed furnaces. The place and time for the discovery of iron smelting is not known, partly because of the difficulty of distinguishing metal extracted from nickel-containing ores from hot-worked meteoritic iron.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the growth of the ancient civilizations, many ancient technologies resulted from advances in manufacturing. Several of the six classic <a href="/wiki/Simple_machines" class="mw-redirect" title="Simple machines">simple machines</a> were invented in Mesopotamia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Mesopotamians have been credited with the invention of the wheel. The <a href="/wiki/Wheel_and_axle" title="Wheel and axle">wheel and axle</a> mechanism first appeared with the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter&#39;s wheel">potter's wheel</a>, invented in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (modern Iraq) during the 5th millennium BC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Egyptian paper made from <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>, were mass-produced and exported throughout the Mediterranean basin. Early construction techniques used by the Ancient Egyptians made use of bricks composed mainly of clay, sand, silt, and other minerals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Medieval_and_early_modern">Medieval and early modern</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Medieval and early modern"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Stocking_Frame.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Stocking_Frame.jpg/250px-Stocking_Frame.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Stocking_Frame.jpg/500px-Stocking_Frame.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption>A stocking frame at Ruddington Framework Knitters' Museum in <a href="/wiki/Ruddington" title="Ruddington">Ruddington</a>, England</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> witnessed new inventions, innovations in the ways of managing traditional means of production, and economic growth. <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">Papermaking</a>, a 2nd-century Chinese technology, was carried to the Middle East when a group of Chinese papermakers were captured in the 8th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Papermaking technology was spread to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Umayyad conquest of Hispania</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A paper mill was established in Sicily in the 12th century. In Europe the fiber to make pulp for making paper was obtained from linen and cotton rags. <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Townsend_White_Jr." title="Lynn Townsend White Jr.">Lynn Townsend White Jr.</a> credited the spinning wheel with increasing the supply of rags, which led to cheap paper, which was a factor in the development of printing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to the casting of cannon, the <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> came into widespread use in France in the mid 15th century. The blast furnace had been used in China since the 4th century BC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tylecote&#95;1992&#95;13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tylecote_1992-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stocking_frame" title="Stocking frame">stocking frame</a>, which was invented in 1598, increased a knitter's number of knots per minute from 100 to 1000.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="First_and_Second_Industrial_Revolutions">First and Second Industrial Revolutions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First and Second Industrial Revolutions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Powerloom_weaving_in_1835.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Powerloom_weaving_in_1835.jpg/250px-Powerloom_weaving_in_1835.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="162" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Powerloom_weaving_in_1835.jpg/500px-Powerloom_weaving_in_1835.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3215" data-file-height="2077" /></a><figcaption>An 1835 illustration of a <a href="/wiki/Roberts_Loom" title="Roberts Loom">Roberts Loom</a> weaving shed</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> was the transition to new manufacturing processes in Europe and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> from 1760 to the 1830s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This transition included going from <a href="/wiki/Craft_production" title="Craft production">hand production methods</a> to machines, new <a href="/wiki/Chemical_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical manufacturing">chemical manufacturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">iron production</a> processes, the increasing use of <a href="/wiki/Steam_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam power">steam power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Water power">water power</a>, the development of <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a> and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">mechanized</a> <a href="/wiki/Factory_system" title="Factory system">factory system</a>. The Industrial Revolution also led to an unprecedented rise in the rate of population growth. Textiles were the dominant industry of the Industrial Revolution in terms of employment, value of output and <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> invested. The <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> was also the first to use modern production methods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-David&#95;S.&#95;Landes&#95;1969&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 40">&#58;&#8202;40&#8202;</span></sup> Rapid industrialization first began in Britain, starting with mechanized spinning in the 1780s,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with high rates of growth in steam power and iron production occurring after 1800. <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">Mechanized textile production</a> spread from Great Britain to continental Europe and the United States in the early 19th century, with important centres of textiles, iron and coal emerging <a href="#Belgium">in Belgium</a> and the United States and later textiles in France.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-David&#95;S.&#95;Landes&#95;1969&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An economic recession occurred from the late 1830s to the early 1840s when the adoption of the Industrial Revolution's early innovations, such as mechanized spinning and weaving, slowed down and their markets matured. Innovations developed late in the period, such as the increasing adoption of locomotives, steamboats and steamships, <a href="/wiki/Hot_blast" title="Hot blast">hot blast iron smelting</a> and new technologies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_telegraph" title="Electrical telegraph">electrical telegraph</a>, were widely introduced in the 1840s and 1850s, were not powerful enough to drive high rates of growth. Rapid economic growth began to occur after 1870, springing from a new group of innovations in what has been called the <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a>. These innovations included new <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">steel making processes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mass-production" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass-production">mass-production</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly lines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a> systems, the large-scale manufacture of machine tools and the use of increasingly advanced machinery in steam-powered factories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-David&#95;S.&#95;Landes&#95;1969&#95;25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Building on improvements in vacuum pumps and materials research, <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulbs</a> became practical for general use in the late 1870s. This invention had a profound effect on the workplace because factories could now have second and third shift workers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shoe production was mechanized during the mid 19th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mass production of <a href="/wiki/Sewing_machine" title="Sewing machine">sewing machines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_machinery" title="Agricultural machinery">agricultural machinery</a> such as reapers occurred in the mid to late 19th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hounshell-1984&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hounshell-1984-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The mass production of bicycles started in the 1880s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hounshell-1984&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hounshell-1984-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Steam-powered factories became widespread, although the conversion from water power to steam occurred in England earlier than in the U.S.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Modern_manufacturing">Modern manufacturing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern manufacturing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Airacobra_P39_Assembly_LOC_02902u.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Airacobra_P39_Assembly_LOC_02902u.jpg/250px-Airacobra_P39_Assembly_LOC_02902u.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Airacobra_P39_Assembly_LOC_02902u.jpg/500px-Airacobra_P39_Assembly_LOC_02902u.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4345" data-file-height="3498" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bell_Aircraft" title="Bell Aircraft">Bell Aircraft</a>'s assembly plant in <a href="/wiki/Wheatfield,_New_York" title="Wheatfield, New York">Wheatfield, New York</a> in 1944</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">Electrification</a> of factories, which had begun gradually in the 1890s after the introduction of the practical <a href="/wiki/DC_motor" title="DC motor">DC motor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/AC_motor" title="AC motor">AC motor</a>, was fastest between 1900 and 1930. This was aided by the establishment of electric utilities with central stations and the lowering of electricity prices from 1914 to 1917.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jerome&#95;1934&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jerome_1934-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">Electric motors</a> allowed more flexibility in manufacturing and required less maintenance than line shafts and belts. Many factories witnessed a 30% increase in output owing to the increasing shift to electric motors. Electrification enabled modern mass production, and the biggest impact of early mass production was in the manufacturing of everyday items, such as at the <a href="/wiki/Ball_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ball Brothers">Ball Brothers</a> <a href="/wiki/Ball_Corporation" title="Ball Corporation">Glass Manufacturing Company</a>, which electrified its <a href="/wiki/Mason_jar" title="Mason jar">mason jar</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Muncie,_Indiana" title="Muncie, Indiana">Muncie, Indiana</a>, U.S. around 1900. The new automated process used glass blowing machines to replace 210 craftsman glass blowers and helpers. A small electric truck was now used to handle 150 dozen bottles at a time whereas previously used hand trucks could only carry 6 dozen bottles at a time. Electric mixers replaced men with shovels handling sand and other ingredients that were fed into the glass furnace. An electric overhead crane replaced 36 <a href="/wiki/Day_labor" title="Day labor">day laborers</a> for moving heavy loads across the factory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mass production was popularized in the late 1910s and 1920s by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hounshell-1984&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hounshell-1984-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which introduced electric motors to the then-well-known technique of chain or sequential production. Ford also bought or designed and built special purpose machine tools and fixtures such as multiple spindle <a href="/wiki/Drill#Drill_press" title="Drill">drill presses</a> that could drill every hole on one side of an engine block in one operation and a multiple head <a href="/wiki/Milling_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Milling machine">milling machine</a> that could simultaneously machine 15 engine blocks held on a single fixture. All of these machine tools were arranged systematically in the production flow and some had special carriages for rolling heavy items into machining positions. Production of the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Model_T" title="Ford Model T">Ford Model T</a> used 32,000 machine tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hounshell1984p288&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hounshell1984p288-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lean_manufacturing" title="Lean manufacturing">Lean manufacturing</a>, also known as just-in-time manufacturing, was developed in Japan in the 1930s. It is a production method aimed primarily at reducing times within the production system as well as response times from suppliers and to customers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Shingo&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shingo-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was introduced in Australia in the 1950s by the British Motor Corporation (Australia) at its Victoria Park plant in Sydney, from where the idea later migrated to Toyota.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> News spread to western countries from Japan in 1977 in two English-language articles: one referred to the methodology as the "Ohno system", after <a href="/wiki/Taiichi_Ohno" title="Taiichi Ohno">Taiichi Ohno</a>, who was instrumental in its development within Toyota.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The other article, by Toyota authors in an international journal, provided additional details.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Finally, those and other publicity were translated into implementations, beginning in 1980 and then quickly multiplying throughout the industry in the United States and other countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="WCM"></span>
The concept of <b>world-class manufacturing</b> is associated with excellence in the manufacturing field.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The term has been promoted by author Richard J. Schonberger,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although Flynn <i>et al.</i> note that the term was initially used by R H Hayes and <a href="/wiki/Steven_C._Wheelwright" title="Steven C. Wheelwright">Steven C. Wheelwright</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> before being taken up by Schonberger.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Machinery_relocation_and_logistics">Machinery relocation and logistics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Machinery relocation and logistics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In many manufacturing industries, production requires not only raw material supply chains but also the transportation and installation of large industrial machinery. Heavy equipment such as presses, injection molding machines, and CNC tools often need to be moved during plant upgrades, facility relocations, or decommissioning projects. These processes involve specialized logistics, including rigging, tilt-and-load transport, forklifts, and oversize-load permitting. In North America, industry associations such as the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.scranet.org/">Specialized Carriers &amp; Rigging Association (SC&amp;RA)</a> provide safety standards and best practices for heavy transport and machinery moving. Independent guides on machinery relocation planning offer strategies for minimizing downtime, such as phasing moves, off-hours relocation, buffer inventory, and detailed site assessments to maintain production continuity during equipment moving.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Manufacturing_strategy">Manufacturing strategy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Manufacturing strategy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>According to a "traditional" view of manufacturing strategy, there are five key dimensions along which the performance of manufacturing can be assessed: cost, <a href="/wiki/Quality_(business)" title="Quality (business)">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dependability" title="Dependability">dependability</a>, flexibility and <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In regard to manufacturing performance, <a href="/wiki/Wickham_Skinner" title="Wickham Skinner">Wickham Skinner</a>, who has been called "the father of <a class="mw-selflink-fragment" href="#Manufacturing_strategy">manufacturing strategy</a>",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> adopted the concept of "focus",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with an implication that a business cannot perform at the highest level along all five dimensions and must therefore select one or two competitive priorities. This view led to the theory of "trade offs" in manufacturing strategy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wass&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wass-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similarly, Elizabeth Haas wrote in 1987 about the delivery of value in manufacturing for customers in terms of "lower prices, greater service responsiveness or higher quality".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The theory of "trade offs" has subsequently being debated and questioned,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wass&#95;51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wass-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Colin New noted that by 1991 it had become "somewhat fashionable" to abandon the trade-off approach,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> whereas Skinner wrote in 1992 that at that time "enthusiasm for the concepts of 'manufacturing strategy' [had] been higher", noting that in <a href="/wiki/Academic_paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Academic paper">academic papers</a>, executive courses and <a href="/wiki/Case_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Case studies">case studies</a>, levels of interest were "bursting out all over".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Manufacturing writer Terry Hill has commented that manufacturing is often seen as a less "strategic" business activity than functions such as marketing and <a href="/wiki/Strategic_financial_management" title="Strategic financial management">finance</a>, and that manufacturing managers have "come late" to business strategy-making discussions, where, as a result, they make only a reactive contribution.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ken Platts and Mike Gregory devised a manufacturing audit scheme intended to support the process of formulating a manufacturing strategy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was popularised in <i>Competitive Manufacturing: A Practical Approach to the Development of a Manufacturing Strategy</i> in 1998, whose publication was supported by the UK's Department of Trade and Industry.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Industrial_policy">Industrial policy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Industrial policy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_policy" title="Industrial policy">Industrial policy</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Economics_of_manufacturing">Economics of manufacturing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Economics of manufacturing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CNT-FAI_Cooperative_Barcelona.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/CNT-FAI_Cooperative_Barcelona.jpg/250px-CNT-FAI_Cooperative_Barcelona.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/CNT-FAI_Cooperative_Barcelona.jpg/500px-CNT-FAI_Cooperative_Barcelona.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="382" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/CNT-FAI" class="mw-redirect" title="CNT-FAI">CNT-FAI</a> worker <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> producing wood and steel products</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Emerging_technologies" title="Emerging technologies">Emerging technologies</a> have offered new growth methods in advanced manufacturing employment opportunities, for example in the <a href="/wiki/Rust_Belt" title="Rust Belt">Manufacturing Belt</a> in the United States. Manufacturing provides important material support for national infrastructure and also for <a href="/wiki/Defense_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense (military)">national defense</a>.
</p><p>On the other hand, most manufacturing processes may involve significant social and environmental costs. The clean-up costs of <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_waste" title="Hazardous waste">hazardous waste</a>, for example, may outweigh the benefits of a product that creates it. Hazardous materials may expose workers to health risks. These costs are now well known and there is effort to address them by improving <a href="/wiki/Efficiency" title="Efficiency">efficiency</a>, reducing waste, using <a href="/wiki/Industrial_symbiosis" title="Industrial symbiosis">industrial symbiosis</a>, and eliminating harmful chemicals.
</p><p>The negative costs of manufacturing can also be addressed legally. Developed countries regulate manufacturing activity with <a href="/wiki/Labor_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor laws">labor laws</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_policy" title="Environmental policy">environmental</a> laws. Across the globe, manufacturers can be subject to regulations and <a href="/wiki/Ecotax" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecotax">pollution taxes</a> to offset the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental impact of manufacturing">environmental costs of manufacturing activities</a>. Labor unions and <a href="/wiki/Craft_guilds" class="mw-redirect" title="Craft guilds">craft guilds</a> have played a historic role in the negotiation of worker rights and wages. Environment laws and labor protections that are available in developed nations may not be available in the <a href="/wiki/Third_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Third world">third world</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tort_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Tort law">Tort law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Product_liability" title="Product liability">product liability</a> impose additional costs on manufacturing. These are significant dynamics in the ongoing process, occurring over the last few decades, of manufacture-based industries relocating operations to "developing-world" economies where the costs of production are significantly lower than in "developed-world" economies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Finance">Finance</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Finance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>From a financial perspective, the goal of the manufacturing industry is mainly to achieve <a href="/wiki/Cost-benefit_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Cost-benefit analysis">cost benefits</a> per unit produced, which in turn leads to cost reductions in product prices for the market towards <a href="/wiki/End_user" title="End user">end customers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2024)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This relative <a href="/wiki/Cost_reduction" title="Cost reduction">cost reduction</a> towards the market, is how manufacturing firms secure their <a href="/wiki/Profit_margins" class="mw-redirect" title="Profit margins">profit margins</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Safety">Safety</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Safety"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The manufacturing sector is identified as a priority industry by the National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health (NIOSH) within the National Occupational Research Agenda (NORA). This designation facilitates the identification of occupational health and safety challenges and the development of intervention strategies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Manufacturing_and_investment">Manufacturing and investment</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Manufacturing and investment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:KapaAuslUSABRDEngl.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/KapaAuslUSABRDEngl.png/250px-KapaAuslUSABRDEngl.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="162" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/KapaAuslUSABRDEngl.png/500px-KapaAuslUSABRDEngl.png 1.5x" data-file-width="978" data-file-height="633" /></a><figcaption>Capacity use in manufacturing in Germany and the United States</figcaption></figure>
<p>Surveys and analyses of trends and issues in manufacturing and investment around the world focus on such things as:
</p>
<ul><li>The nature and sources of the considerable variations that occur cross-nationally in levels of manufacturing and wider industrial-economic growth;</li>
<li>Competitiveness; and</li>
<li>Attractiveness to foreign direct investors.</li></ul>
<p>In addition to general overviews, researchers have examined the features and factors affecting particular key aspects of manufacturing development. They have compared production and investment in a range of Western and non-Western countries and presented case studies of growth and performance in important individual industries and market-economic sectors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On June 26, 2009, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Immelt" title="Jeff Immelt">Jeff Immelt</a>, the CEO of <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>, called for the United States to increase its manufacturing base employment to 20% of the workforce, commenting that the U.S. has outsourced too much in some areas and can no longer rely on the financial sector and <a href="/wiki/Consumer_spending" title="Consumer spending">consumer spending</a> to drive demand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Immelt&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Immelt-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further, while U.S. manufacturing performs well compared to the rest of the U.S. economy, research shows that it performs poorly compared to manufacturing in other high-wage countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A total of 3.2&#160;million&#160;– one in six U.S. manufacturing jobs&#160;– have disappeared between 2000 and 2007.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the UK, <a href="/wiki/EEF_the_manufacturers_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="EEF the manufacturers organisation">EEF the manufacturers organisation</a> has led calls for the UK economy to be rebalanced to rely less on financial services and has actively promoted the manufacturing agenda.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Major_manufacturing_nations">Major manufacturing nations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Major manufacturing nations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_manufacturing#By_country" title="Outline of manufacturing">Outline of manufacturing §&#160;By country</a></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Industrial_Development_Organization" title="United Nations Industrial Development Organization">United Nations Industrial Development Organization</a> (UNIDO), <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Manufacturing in China">China</a> is the manufacturer with the highest output worldwide in 2023, producing 28.7% of the total global manufacturing output, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_the_United_States" title="Manufacturing in the United States">United States of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Manufacturing in Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Japan" title="Manufacturing in Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Manufacturing in India">India</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-www2.deloitte.com&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www2.deloitte.com-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>UNIDO also publishes a Competitive Industrial Performance (CIP) Index, which measures the competitive manufacturing ability of different nations. The CIP Index combines a nation's gross manufacturing output with other factors like high-tech capability and the nation's impact on the world economy. Germany topped the 2020 CIP Index, followed by China, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_South_Korea" title="Manufacturing in South Korea">South Korea</a>, the United States, and Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2023, the manufacturing industry in the United States accounted for 10.70% of the total national output, employing 8.41% of the workforce. The total value of manufacturing output reached $2.5 trillion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2023, Germany's manufacturing output reached $844.93 billion, marking a 12.25% increase from 2022. The sector employed approximately 5.5 million people, accounting for around 20.8% of the workforce.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="List_of_countries_by_manufacturing_output">List of countries by manufacturing output</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Manufacturing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: List of countries by manufacturing output"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

</p>
